  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_dbae5fce-ce03-430e-bb96-7f53f5cac9c6'

<span id="freeTextContainer4212374922915433313"><i>The Song of Power</i> opened the gateway to the Realm of the Sidhe, allowing young Michael Perrin to slip through. Now Michael faces years of captivity and deadly struggles for the future of the Realm and of Earth--leading finally to a terrible confrontation on the streets of Los Angeles, with the soul of humanity at stake.<br/><br/>Weaving the power of music, poetry, and myth into a he</span>
  <span id="freeText4212374922915433313" style="display:none"><i>The Song of Power</i> opened the gateway to the Realm of the Sidhe, allowing young Michael Perrin to slip through. Now Michael faces years of captivity and deadly struggles for the future of the Realm and of Earth--leading finally to a terrible confrontation on the streets of Los Angeles, with the soul of humanity at stake.<br /><br />Weaving the power of music, poetry, and myth into a headlong narrative of nearly overwhelming intensity, <i>Song of Earth and Power</i> is one of the most original fantasy epics of our time, a vast tapestry of relentless suspense, terrible beauty, and brilliant imagination. Originally published years ago in two parts, it now returns in a new edition rewritten by the author and published in a single volume as he originally intended. Wrote<i> Analog</i> on its original appearance: "A delight....A vision of Faery that may owe a bit to a wish to do it right. Read it."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4212374922915433313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_173566178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/173566178" />
    <a title="Katy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5552109-katy"><img alt="Katy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1405580498p2/5552109.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/173566178?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katy" href="/user/show/5552109-katy">Katy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of epic fantasy, magic realism, Sidhe, alternate realities</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer173566178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1862476912069673864">"The Infinity Concerto" - Book One in the Songs of Earth and Power omnibus - is multi-layered and textured. I believe it will likely take me several readings to find all the meanings that are embedded in this story. The basic storyline revolves around Michael Perrin, a thoughtful young man who wants to be a poet. He befriends a composer named Arno Waltiri, who it is said wrote a concerto called The Infinity Concerto that was so unusual that it not only drove its listeners mad but also that many </span>
  <span id="freeText1862476912069673864" style="display:none">"The Infinity Concerto" - Book One in the Songs of Earth and Power omnibus - is multi-layered and textured. I believe it will likely take me several readings to find all the meanings that are embedded in this story. The basic storyline revolves around Michael Perrin, a thoughtful young man who wants to be a poet. He befriends a composer named Arno Waltiri, who it is said wrote a concerto called The Infinity Concerto that was so unusual that it not only drove its listeners mad but also that many of those listeners disappeared. Waltiri claims that the concerto was primarily inspired by a man called David Clarkham, who subsequently disappeared; Arno gives Michael a key and a piece of paper with directions to follow, that should lead him to Clarkham. Waltiri dies soon thereafter. Despite warnings from Waltiri's wife Golda that Waltiri repented of his choice to give these to him, Michael decides to go ahead and follow the directions - and ends up somewhere . . . that is not Earth anymore. Forced to learn to survive, Michael has to grow up and grow strong very quickly - but is he just a pawn in some hidden power struggle? Or is he something else altogether?<br /><br />Some sections of this part of the text seemed rather slow moving, but everything was necessary for the plot. Because the plot is so intricate, at times ponderous prose is necessary to bring about all the necessary information. All-in-all, I found book one quite enjoyable.<br /><br />The Serpent Mage" - book two - picks up shortly after Infinity Concerto leaves off. Michael Perrin is back home, living with his parents and continuing his training. Arno Waltiri has left his estate and the disposition of his papers and recordings to Michael. Waltiri has also left his house to Michael and eventually Michael moves in and begins to go through the papers.<br /><br />Michael's desires for normality are shattered, however, when he reads a news story about strange bodies discovered in a nearby hotel - one grossly obese, one strangely mummified and in a party dress. Other news stories speak of "hauntings" around the world - Michael suspects that the Sidhe are coming to Earth. If that isn't enough, he is contacted by a musical faculty member from UCLA named Kristine Pendeers who is looking for the Infinity Concerto - Opus 45. She wants to discover and perform it; and she has a friend who, with the help of letters and papers they hope to discover in Waltiri's estate, hopes to finish Mahler's unfinished Symphony. And they then plan play the two pieces together. Once the decision is made to start looking for these materials, Michael begins to fall under various attacks to stop him from completing these tasks.<br /><br />Hopefully the bits of plot I outlined above don't spoil the book for anyone - I could hardly outline less without being so vague about the basic plot of the book as to be basically providing you with a meaningless synopsis; however, there is so much more to this book than the above. Greg Bear weaves throughout this story a fascinating new mythos about the creation and evolution, de-evolution and re-evolution of man and the universe that I found to be quite astonishing in its depth and breadth. He weaves in references to several world religions and ties them in to his mythos, showing how the original truth was "twisted" over the years to conform to what would best serve those in power. It's a really interesting device and I enjoyed the way it was woven in throughout the story. You may also look at vegetarianism in a whole new light.<br /><br />There was only one thing about the story that bothered me and I'm not sure if it was because I misinterpreted what I was reading or if it is because of some sort of misogyny on the part of the author. It is mentioned several times throughout the course of the book that "magic is carried by the woman." However, not one single mage shown is a woman. If women carry the magic, why aren't there any female mages? Or, as I said, perhaps I am misinterpreting it, and by "carry" they mean like a recessive gene - they carry the magic, but cannot use it.<br /><br />Those who are fans of epic fantasy, magical realism, stories of the Sidhe (especially of the darker natures thereof) or simply well-crafted alternate realities, please do not miss this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1862476912069673864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26948477" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26948477" />
    <a title="Jake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/972904-jake"><img alt="Jake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230756051p2/972904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26948477?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jake" href="/user/show/972904-jake">Jake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26948477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4219941135438366784">I was lent this book by my assistant manager, a generally wonderful human being who responded to my complaints about having packed up all my books before moving by lending me this, and several other things as well. I started with this because it’s out of print, belonged to her son, and frankly, looked the most interesting. (The other is a copy of Relic, which was a book before a movie, and several of my co-worker’s seem to like. I may read it eventually, but I’m kinda on a non-fiction kick at th</span>
  <span id="freeText4219941135438366784" style="display:none">I was lent this book by my assistant manager, a generally wonderful human being who responded to my complaints about having packed up all my books before moving by lending me this, and several other things as well. I started with this because it’s out of print, belonged to her son, and frankly, looked the most interesting. (The other is a copy of Relic, which was a book before a movie, and several of my co-worker’s seem to like. I may read it eventually, but I’m kinda on a non-fiction kick at the moment. But I digress)<br /><br />According to Amazon, this was originally actually two separate books, which Bear re-wrote and connected to make one large story. He did a fine job of joining them, since honestly, if I hadn’t looked at Amazon, I would never have known. Songs of Earth and Power tells the story of Michael Perrin, a sixteen year old boy who, thanks to a gift from a dead friend/mentor, wanders into the Realm of the Sidhe, and finds himself caught up the struggles and politics of a foreign world. He spends a fair amount of time traveling and learning about the Sidhe, their Realm, and their magic, before eventually making his way back to LA…at which point, things from the other side start spilling over too.<br /><br />It’s a very interesting book, with some neat ideas, and a very, very, interesting setting The whole structure of the Realm, the nature of the Sidhe and how they interact with humanity, and so on, is thought out and explicated in a way that makes them seem very odd, alien, and well, fantastic. Which is cool. This is not a knock off of Tolkien, Narnia, or any other fantasy world, and seems to have a lot of roots in authentic mythologies, in feeling, if not in actual fact. I found the character’s not quite as engaging as the world, however…Michael is interesting, and is not horrifically angsty for a teenage protagonist (though he does have his moments), but a lot of the other characters are less memorable. As I think of it, the only one’s that really stick out are Nikolai, a Russian fisherman who shows up halfway through the story, and the Crane Women, who are a set of three half-Sidhe crones that train and mentor Michael.<br /><br />The book is out of print now, but it’s definitely worth tracking down a copy if you’re a fantasy fan. Bear has some really neat ideas, and plays them out in some very interesting ways. The book drags a little in the middle while Michael stops for a training sequence, but once he gets moving, the book picks back up again too. Worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4219941135438366784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400620321?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Koen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Koen" href="/user/show/12215616-koen">Koen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400620321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14204235037166961495">“Songs of Earth and Power” comprises of two stories by Greg Bear: “The Infinity Concerto” and “The Serpent Mage”. The original story is from 1984 and this edition of 1992 has an Afterword added by the author. <br/><br/>I’ve read the first book in Dutch a long time ago and could never find the Dutch version of The Serpent Mage and therefore stuck with the feeling that the story wasn’t over yet. Early May of this year, we went to the SF Convention at the Antwerp Expo and found this edition with both books i</span>
  <span id="freeText14204235037166961495" style="display:none">“Songs of Earth and Power” comprises of two stories by Greg Bear: “The Infinity Concerto” and “The Serpent Mage”. The original story is from 1984 and this edition of 1992 has an Afterword added by the author. <br /><br />I’ve read the first book in Dutch a long time ago and could never find the Dutch version of The Serpent Mage and therefore stuck with the feeling that the story wasn’t over yet. Early May of this year, we went to the SF Convention at the Antwerp Expo and found this edition with both books in one.<br />I started again with the first book reading about the adventures of Michael Perrin and his encounter with Arno Waltiri and his wife Golda. Later on in the book I met David Clarkham and a lot of other characters defining the story as it is: A young man switching from our known world to the other side, the realm of the Shide. The main stories always seem to come back to classical music and poetry. In the second book you get to meet Kristine Pendeers, she is also the subject of the added Afterword of the author in the 1992 edition.<br />Enjoy these two books and keep in mind that the first book is somewhat slow to start as the second book runs in a perfect pace to the end: You can’t stop reading ;-)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14204235037166961495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer297151780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5945522934618100558">I loved it. Were I inclined, I could have looked up/listened to all the concertos mentioned in the first part and garnered a deeper appreciation for classical music. I passed on that, though, because the unfolding mystery intrigued me. Come on, otherworldy beings appearing by the boy's bed in the night, discussing things much like doctors gathered around a patient's bed?<br/><br/>Others have mentioned skipping "The Infinity...", but without that you may not entirely appreciate what the protagonist is expe</span>
  <span id="freeText5945522934618100558" style="display:none">I loved it. Were I inclined, I could have looked up/listened to all the concertos mentioned in the first part and garnered a deeper appreciation for classical music. I passed on that, though, because the unfolding mystery intrigued me. Come on, otherworldy beings appearing by the boy's bed in the night, discussing things much like doctors gathered around a patient's bed?<br /><br />Others have mentioned skipping "The Infinity...", but without that you may not entirely appreciate what the protagonist is experiencing. From a typical teen mentality, well, a bit better, being versed in such music, but still with the hormonal maturity one that age suffers, to the frustrations of growing up hard and fast (faster than you know), this novel is well written, so that you can, to a degree at least, appreciate his trials and tribulations that are so necessary to his survival in the harsh world in which he finds himself. I say 'to a degree' because the forms of magic and magical creatures he deals with are not what we certainly deal with in our daily lives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5945522934618100558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4825046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1240195573262683687">The death of Kristine<br/><br/>In my edition there is a rather odd postscript added by the author about a girl called Kristine. He wrote about her death, she then died in real life and he felt and appears to still feel great pangs of lumpen guilt, so he has installed her as the interesting yet somewhat peripheral female love interest in the Serpent Mage.<br/><br/>I strongly recommend that you read the second of these two (The Serpent Mage), without reading the Infinity Concerto. In the second, all of the references</span>
  <span id="freeText1240195573262683687" style="display:none">The death of Kristine<br /><br />In my edition there is a rather odd postscript added by the author about a girl called Kristine. He wrote about her death, she then died in real life and he felt and appears to still feel great pangs of lumpen guilt, so he has installed her as the interesting yet somewhat peripheral female love interest in the Serpent Mage.<br /><br />I strongly recommend that you read the second of these two (The Serpent Mage), without reading the Infinity Concerto. In the second, all of the references back provide splendid depth and background, but if you actually read them, they are terribly plodding. <br /><br />(The Many Coloured Land is another powerful series, where you lose nothing at all by dropping the very first one, especially as the author provides a copious summary at the start of the next.)<br /><br /><br /><br />Last time I read this book I wrote:<br /><br />Music and the Sidhe, failing in their own creation and returning home.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11719959249849425295">I have kind of a love/hate relationship with 'urban' fantasy. Of all the possible genres in fiction, it is hands down my favorite. But for whatever reason, it's also the order and species of writing within which I have the hardest time finding a <i>truly</i> satisfying story. Even my go-to guy, Charles De Lint (who I love, and no mistake), will more often than not leave me itching for something just barely more... <i>something</i>. I don't know if I'll ever be able to articulate exactly what it is I'm looking</span>
  <span id="freeText11719959249849425295" style="display:none">I have kind of a love/hate relationship with 'urban' fantasy. Of all the possible genres in fiction, it is hands down my favorite. But for whatever reason, it's also the order and species of writing within which I have the hardest time finding a <i>truly</i> satisfying story. Even my go-to guy, Charles De Lint (who I love, and no mistake), will more often than not leave me itching for something just barely more... <i>something</i>. I don't know if I'll ever be able to articulate exactly what it is I'm looking for, I just know that it's a rare thing that I actually find it.<br /><br />In <i>Songs of Earth and Power</i>, I found it in spades. Originally two books- <i>The Infinity Concerto</i> and its sequel, <i>The Serpent Mage</i>- <br /><br />Hee hee... I got called away! Finish this in a bit.<br />I know you're all dying to see how it all ends! <br /><br />:P<br /><br /></span>

        <a title="Healing Toolbox Bruce Dickson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Healing Toolbox Bruce Dickson" href="/user/show/13698964-healing-toolbox-bruce-dickson">Healing Toolbox Bruce Dickson</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3465642468" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13110961941531597978">Songs of Earth and Power 1 & 2 - Review of both books, both parts one and two read together and revised by the author.<br/><br/>If you liked Greg's Eternity, you will likely enjoy this two-book series<br/><br/>The first act, getting to the Faere realm, is pure original Spielberg. Wonderful. Then it becomes a fable of personal becoming--set in two worlds, Earth and Faere. To say more, a fable of becoming more self-masterful, set in fantasy tropes yet written by a hard science fiction author. From another reviewer he</span>
  <span id="freeText13110961941531597978" style="display:none">Songs of Earth and Power 1 &amp; 2 - Review of both books, both parts one and two read together and revised by the author.<br /><br />If you liked Greg's Eternity, you will likely enjoy this two-book series<br /><br />The first act, getting to the Faere realm, is pure original Spielberg. Wonderful. Then it becomes a fable of personal becoming--set in two worlds, Earth and Faere. To say more, a fable of becoming more self-masterful, set in fantasy tropes yet written by a hard science fiction author. From another reviewer here: "There are times during this story that one can't help but contemplate their own Human potential." Paraphrase from the book, "Compacency is the greatest enemy of [anyone wishing to claim greater self-mastery].<br /><br />Not a crowd pleasing book because of too few predictable fictional tropes. Sure, the skeleton is indeed, "teenager finds himself and matures due to sequential challenges in a fantasy world." However Greg is constantly subverting this tired trope to dig deeper into what it means to be human, maturing out of complacent imagination, the dangers and temptations of fantasy. The teenage boy self is sacrificed with a authorly fervor I found refreshing. <br /><br />Not a crowd pleaser in this sense too: At several places, the story stops or loses all momentum. The main character is simply present with himself with no obvious purpose or direction. In this way the story is much more introverted and mystical than Star Wars fare. <br /><br />If you liked the depth and luxury of original thought in Greg's Eternity, you will likely enjoy these two books too.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13110961941531597978" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2279481497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17127701109308987870">Gosh, this book took me forever to read. It's not because it wasn't interesting, but I kept falling asleep! That being said - yes, it was a really good book. I was just tired. ;)<br/><br/>Michael Perrin is a 16 year old boy who gets transported to The Realm and learns that he has a bit of magic. He starts out as any other teenage boy does - a bit whiny and annoying - but the nice part is that you actually get to see him grow throughout the book. You see him go from the whiny, "I just want to go home and w</span>
  <span id="freeText17127701109308987870" style="display:none">Gosh, this book took me forever to read. It's not because it wasn't interesting, but I kept falling asleep! That being said - yes, it was a really good book. I was just tired. ;)<br /><br />Michael Perrin is a 16 year old boy who gets transported to The Realm and learns that he has a bit of magic. He starts out as any other teenage boy does - a bit whiny and annoying - but the nice part is that you actually get to see him grow throughout the book. You see him go from the whiny, "I just want to go home and write my poetry" kid to a man who realizes that he's got responsibilities and a job to do. I liked that you could tell when he realized that he wasn't a kid anymore.<br /><br />This book had me laughing and feeling sorry for some characters, but I liked that there weren't really any characters that I didn't like at all. There were some that made me go, "Well, they're pointless - why are they here again?" but otherwise they were all pretty enjoyable. The Crane Women were my favorite characters by far. They were funny and a bit whimsical and it made you want to learn all you could from them. Overall, it was a very interesting book and a good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17127701109308987870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3414579035" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali" href="/user/show/4891239-ali">Ali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=good-read">good-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4891239-ali?shelf=read-and-loved-many-years-ago">read-and-loved-many-years-ago</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3414579035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15497685533227895595">I read this as two separate books, and once I'd read the first one I had to read the second to complete the tale, so publishing it as one book is probably sensible. The first book sets up the tale of another world where Sidhe, Breeds (Sidhe/human) and human live in an uneasy and unequal pact. The focus is on Michael, a 16 year old human who by another's design makes his way over to this world and is caught up in the politics, drama, religion and sheer brutality and beauty of this other. The othe</span>
  <span id="freeText15497685533227895595" style="display:none">I read this as two separate books, and once I'd read the first one I had to read the second to complete the tale, so publishing it as one book is probably sensible. The first book sets up the tale of another world where Sidhe, Breeds (Sidhe/human) and human live in an uneasy and unequal pact. The focus is on Michael, a 16 year old human who by another's design makes his way over to this world and is caught up in the politics, drama, religion and sheer brutality and beauty of this other. The otherness is bewitching, the horror pretty damn horrifying and Michael's journey fascinating. The second book is a perhaps a more compelling read as so much that was set up in the first book comes into play and begins to make more sense, plus a lot of it takes place in our world and there is always a sense of wonder and frisson when the magical impacts on the mundane and normal. :-) This is a wonderful work of fantasy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15497685533227895595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1675482798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1675482798" />
    <a title="Nathan Dehoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17551422-nathan-dehoff"><img alt="Nathan Dehoff" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1675482798?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan Dehoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan Dehoff" href="/user/show/17551422-nathan-dehoff">Nathan Dehoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1675482798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8993166403299905602">This is actually a set of two different books, <i>The Infinity Concerto</i> and <i>The Serpent Mage</i>. While primarily known as a science fiction author, these books are more straight fantasy. The poet Michael Perrin befriends a composer named Arno Waltiri, and ends up in the realm of the Sidhe. The first book mostly consists of Michael exploring the realm, and eventually completing Coleridge's <i>Kubla Khan</i> in his own way in order to defeat a mage. In the second book, Michael returns to Earth and becomes the </span>
  <span id="freeText8993166403299905602" style="display:none">This is actually a set of two different books, <i>The Infinity Concerto</i> and <i>The Serpent Mage</i>. While primarily known as a science fiction author, these books are more straight fantasy. The poet Michael Perrin befriends a composer named Arno Waltiri, and ends up in the realm of the Sidhe. The first book mostly consists of Michael exploring the realm, and eventually completing Coleridge's <i>Kubla Khan</i> in his own way in order to defeat a mage. In the second book, Michael returns to Earth and becomes the caretaker for Waltiri's collection. This leads to him organizing a performance of Waltiri's magical concerto, which results in the two worlds being merged. He also finds out that Mozart and Mahler are still alive in the fairy world. As you can tell, in addition to dropping references to earlier mythology and fairy lore, Bear makes music and poetry integral parts of his plot. As with the <i>Eon</i> books, the author makes his world so vast that we only see a little part of it with the protagonist. Also, there was some gratuitous sex thrown in. It seemed that, for every woman Michael met, either he was attracted to her or her to him, if not both. It didn't bother me; it just seems like this might be a quirk of the author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8993166403299905602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15172800778092726581">I was completely enthralled by these two books and it had nothing to do with style or substance but with story that galloped along just out of reach and trapped me in its clutches somehow until I was transported to another realm. How a book does that, how a reader does that in reading a book, is a total mystery to me. Rating my experience is pointless and I think each individual reader will have to try it and see if the magic happens for them. When it does work as this book did for me, playing k</span>
  <span id="freeText15172800778092726581" style="display:none">I was completely enthralled by these two books and it had nothing to do with style or substance but with story that galloped along just out of reach and trapped me in its clutches somehow until I was transported to another realm. How a book does that, how a reader does that in reading a book, is a total mystery to me. Rating my experience is pointless and I think each individual reader will have to try it and see if the magic happens for them. When it does work as this book did for me, playing knowledgable critic surely ruins the private experience by bringing logic and measure into it. <br /><br />This is true of any reading experience but especially of reading Fantasy. I read mostly Science Fiction but I keep putting the few Fantasy Books I have read onto my Favorites shelf, so I must be drawn in that direction. Mainly it is the good stories that draw me in and I lose awareness of the language unless it is atrocious or particularly fine. The language says "look at me" and can be very impressive. The story says "follow me" and, if everything is just right, I follow.<br /></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17427699982521545133">Excellent idea. Could use some editing. <br/>I found the premise of this to be pretty cool. Ancient intelligence, alternative realities, Power of music and magic. There is A LOT going on here. I enjoyed the story and felt like main character was fully developed. However I found the narration to be a bit tedious. Like I said, there is a lot going on here. At times he tends to brush over the background of the story, or veil it behind a long poem myths. I think if more could have been said with fewer wo</span>
  <span id="freeText17427699982521545133" style="display:none">Excellent idea. Could use some editing. <br />I found the premise of this to be pretty cool. Ancient intelligence, alternative realities, Power of music and magic. There is A LOT going on here. I enjoyed the story and felt like main character was fully developed. However I found the narration to be a bit tedious. Like I said, there is a lot going on here. At times he tends to brush over the background of the story, or veil it behind a long poem myths. I think if more could have been said with fewer words, it would have been much easier to understand what was going on. Not that I want everything spelled out too obviously, I do like a challenge, but parts of this were just confusing. I'm not going to read a book 2 or 3 times looking for deep meaning, or hidden allegory. There were long sections that slowed down the narration at the expense of filling out some of the other characters and story line. This has some very cool parts and was worth it in the end. But still, my least favorite Greg Bear book so far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17427699982521545133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3669096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17541905165434631310">From this book, I learned that it is possible for even the most brilliant book to go so thoroughly out of print that it is never seen or heard from again. I only managed to read it and it's sequal, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/774416.The_Serpent_Mage' title='The Serpent Mage by Greg Bear' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Serpent Mage</a>, because they were lent to me by a friend. I've been looking off and on ever since, but I haven't <br/><br/>The book begins with a mystery, so there isn't much I can say. It's an intense story, a modern fairy tale in the truest sense of the term, a coming of age story set against a backdrop of</span>
  <span id="freeText17541905165434631310" style="display:none">From this book, I learned that it is possible for even the most brilliant book to go so thoroughly out of print that it is never seen or heard from again. I only managed to read it and it's sequal, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/774416.The_Serpent_Mage" title="The Serpent Mage by Greg Bear" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Serpent Mage</a>, because they were lent to me by a friend. I've been looking off and on ever since, but I haven't <br /><br />The book begins with a mystery, so there isn't much I can say. It's an intense story, a modern fairy tale in the truest sense of the term, a coming of age story set against a backdrop of cosmic conflict. It's a shame that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16024.Greg_Bear" title="Greg Bear" rel="nofollow noopener"> Greg Bear</a> stopped writing fantasy; he was good at it. I recommend this book wholeheartedly to anyone.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('47dxpN938SC5WPqMXnJoS3IyYaRTjTc6h1Hbpnkc29ofC7lc+S/t/yO6hRp2dI31ATe+PAcKIlIlMs2ndH2mQw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YMH1ptoxG+RJBzmWuwbIsJKDghxkOyVC8NAKeGPnSuecfT1e/GkHO9PlRgCTAC0O4YZdhDC8MCpSsxx5boY3fg==')})
  }

He sold his first short story, at the age of fifteen, to Robert Lowndes's Famous Science Fiction. <br/><br/>A full-time writer, he lives in Washington State with his family. He is married to Astrid Anderson Bear. He is the son-in-law of Poul Anderson.
